[1] Chapter 1 The White-Haired Man
[2] At around midnight, a man was walking through the Place de la Concorde (note: the largest square in Paris, located on the right bank of the Seine River, in the northwestern part of the city). Despite the fur coat that covered his emaciated body, it was easy to see his weakness and poverty. This was a small man with a rat-like face. One could say that such a person would never draw attention or achieve anything significant. However, in making such a conclusion, the onlookers may have made a mistake. For although he seemed so small and insignificant, he could play an important role in changing the world. Even in a kingdom ruled by rats, he would be the king of all rats.
[3] At this moment, an important task awaited him at home. But before going home, he had to make a deal that had nothing to do with his task. His face, illuminated by moonlight, became paler and more distinct: a thin nose with a slight curve. His father was an old tailor, a Jew from the Netherlands. He was still abroad that night, completing a transaction that his father loved.
[4] He came to the Seine River, crossed the bridge, and entered a notorious neighborhood in Paris. He stopped in front of a large, unguarded building and climbed the stairs to an apartment on the fourth floor. Before he could knock, a woman opened the door, as if waiting for him. She said nothing, silently helped him out of his coat, and led him into a tacky living room. The light bulbs were covered in dirty pink floral decorations, but the woman's rough makeup and distinct Mongolian features were still visible under the light.
[5] "Is it all done, little one?" she asked.
[6] "It's all done, Boris Ivanovich," he replied.
[7] He nodded and lowered his voice: "I don't think anyone is following me."
[8] But his voice betrayed his fear. He walked to the window, gently pulled the curtains aside, and looked out for a moment before turning back and saying:
[9] "There are two people on the sidewalk across the street. It looks like…" He stopped speaking, biting his fingernails in thought, a habit he had when he was afraid.
[10] "There are two people on the sidewalk across the street. It looks like…" He stopped speaking, biting his fingernails in thought, a habit he had when he was afraid.
[11] The Russian woman, however, simply shook her head nonchalantly.
[12] "They were there before you came…"
[13] "Timing doesn't mean anything. In my opinion, they're watching this building."
[14] "Possibly," she agreed.
[15] "If that's the case…"
[16] "So what? Even if they sniffed something out, the person they're tracking won't be you. This is just their starting point."
[17] A smirk of sarcasm appeared on the man's lips.
[18] "You're right." The man admitted.
[19] He pondered for a couple of minutes before speaking softly: "This damn American is more protective than anyone else."
[20] "Indeed."
[21] He walked back to the window.
[22] "What a troublesome customer!" he muttered with a cold smile. "I guess the police are already on his tail. Well, well, I wish you two scoundrels the best of luck!"
[23] Olga Dmitrovna shook her head.
[24] "If that American is really as they say, then even two scoundrels won't be a match for him." She paused for a moment, "I was thinking…"
[25] "What?"
[26] "Nothing. Just that tonight someone passed by this street twice, an old man with white hair."
[27] "So what?"
[28] "Well, when he passed by these two, he dropped one of his gloves on purpose, and one of them picked it up and gave it back to the old man with white hair. It's such an old trick."
[29] "So you think that the old man with white hair is the employer of these two guys?"
[30] "Kind of."
[31] The Russian seemed to feel terrified and anxious.
[32] "Can you be sure that the package is still safe? Has nothing been touched? Things are getting more and more complicated."
[33] He started to bite his nails again.
[34] "Take a look yourself."
[35] She bent down by the fireplace and skillfully moved the coal blocks aside. Underneath, she took out an oval-shaped package wrapped tightly in greasy newspapers from a pile of disorganized newspapers and handed it to him.
[36] "You're quite clever!" he said, nodding approvingly.
[37] "This house has been searched twice, and my sheets have been torn."
[38] "As I was saying," he muttered, "things are getting more and more complicated. Bickering over prices was a mistake."
[39] He removed the outer layer of the package, revealing a small brown paper parcel. He opened it, glanced at the contents, and then rewrapped it tightly. At that moment, the doorbell rang sharply.
[40] "The Americans have arrived on time," Olga said, glancing at the clock.
[41] She left the room and soon returned with a stranger who was tall and broad-shouldered, clearly an American. His gaze swept over the two people in the room.
[42] "Are you Mr. Krassini?" he asked politely.
[43] "That's right," Boris replied. "Please forgive me, but the meeting location has changed. But the most important thing is to maintain secrecy. I can't carry the goods with me to the meeting."
[44] "Ah, I see," the American said politely.
[45] "You did say that this transaction could only be between us, right? Ensuring absolute safety is one of the key conditions of this deal."
[46] The American nodded.
[47] "We have agreed on this," he said coldly. "Now, perhaps, you should bring out the goods for me to see."
[48] "Did you bring the money? Cash?"
[49] "Yes," the other replied.
[50] But he did not seem to want to take out the money. After a moment's hesitation, Kresnin placed the paper package on the table.
[51] The American opened the paper package, took the contents out, and examined them carefully under the light. He then pulled out a thick leather wallet from his pocket and took out a wad of money, which he handed to the Russian. The Russian took the money, carefully counted the bills.
[52] "Is that right?"
[53] "Thank you, it's correct."
[54] "Excellent!" said the American. He hastily shoved the brown paper package into his pocket, bowed to Olga. "Goodbye, Miss. Goodbye, Mr. Kreisner."
[55] "See you later, Miss. See you later, Mr. Kreisner."
[56] He then left the room, closing the door. The two remaining people looked at each other. The man opened his dry lips and muttered, "I wonder if he'll make it back to his hotel safely?"
[57] The two looked out the window just in time to see the American walking down the street. He turned left and walked away quickly without looking back. The two shadows at the door followed silently behind him. The pursuer and the pursued were both shrouded in the darkness of the night.
[58] Olga Dmitrovna spoke up, "He'll make it home safely. Don't worry about his safety, and don't expect any other surprises."
[59] "Why do you think he'll be safe?" Kresin asked curiously.
[60] "If a person has so much money, he won't be a fool," Olga said. "Since we're talking about money..." she looked meaningfully at Kresin.
[61] "Hmm?"
[62] "What about my share, Boris Ivanovich?"
[63] He reluctantly gave her two bills. She nodded without expression, acknowledging her thanks, and put the money in her sock.
[64] "Very well," she said, satisfied.
[65] He curiously looked at Olga.
[66] "You don't feel sorry, Olga Dmitrovna?"
[67] "Sorry? What's there to be sorry about?"
[68] "You gave up something so valuable. I believe most women would go crazy over something like that."
[69] She nodded.
[70] "You're right. Many women would go crazy for it, but I won't. Right now, I just want to know one thing..."
[71] "What is it?" Kreisner asked curiously.
[72] "This American got hold of these things, and I am confident that both he and the things will be alright in the end. But what will happen afterwards...?"
[73] "What are you thinking about?"
[74] "He will surely give this thing to a woman," Olga pondered, "I wonder what would happen if a woman got her hands on it?"
[75] She began to get impatient and walked over to the window again. Suddenly she let out a gasp and turned her head to her companion.
[76] "Look, the person I just mentioned is walking down the street now."
[77] They both looked out the window to the street below, where a tall, thin man with an air of elegance strolled leisurely. He wore a top hat and a coat. As he passed under a streetlamp, it was clear that he had a white head of hair peeking out from under the top hat.
[78] Chapter 2: The Baron
[79] The white-haired man seemed to care little about his surroundings, walking leisurely on his own path. He hummed a tune, turned right at one intersection, and then left at the next.
[80] Suddenly, he stopped in his tracks, listening intently. He heard a sound that sounded like a tire bursting or a gunshot. A curious smile appeared on his lips, and he continued his leisurely walk.
[81] At the corner, he saw a lively scene: a policeman was jotting something down in a notebook, and a few people who had passed by the scene had gathered around. The gray-haired man mingled with the onlookers, politely asking for information from those around him.
[82] "Excuse me, what happened here?"
[83] "Well, sir, two scoundrels attacked an elderly American."
[84] "Did they hurt the American?"
[85] "Actually, no." The responder chuckled. "The American, he had a revolver in his pocket. The two scoundrels didn't have a chance to act before the American shot at them point-blank. They ran away in a panic, and the police, as usual, arrived late."
[86] "So, huh." The bald man said.
[87] This incident seemed to cause no emotional fluctuations in him.
[88] He calmly continued on his way. Soon, he crossed the Seine River and arrived in the city's upscale district. After walking for about twenty minutes, he came to a quiet street where many upper-class residences were located. He stopped in front of one of the houses.
[89] In front of him was a shop. As a shop, it was quite elegant, understated, and unobtrusive. Its owner was Dr. Papapoulos. As a highly respected antique dealer, Dr. Papapoulos did not need any advertising to attract customers, and in fact, his business rarely took place on the shop's counters. Dr. Papapoulos had a mansion on the Champs-Élysées, and it was more likely to encounter him there at this time of day than in his shop. However, the bald man seemed confidently ringing the doorbell and quickly scanning the empty street behind him.
[90] His confidence seemed to be well-founded. The door of the shop opened a crack, and a man with a golden earring and a dark complexion appeared in the dim light.
[91] "Good evening," said the visitor. "Is your master in?"
[92] "My master is in his room. But he will not receive an unexpected visitor at this time."
[93] "I think he would be willing to see me. Please tell him that his friend, the Count is here."
[94] The doorkeeper opened the door, allowing the bald man to enter and wait in the room.
[95] The man who called himself the Count always spoke with his face covered by a small black silk veil. When the doorkeeper came back to report that his master was willing to see the visitor at this time, the Count's expression subtly changed. The doorkeeper must have been well-trained, as he showed no surprise when he saw the visitor's face covered by a small black silk veil. He simply led the Count to the end of the hall to a room and opened the door with a voice full of respect: "The Count is here."
[96] The figure that rose to welcome the guest was impressive, this was Mr. Papapopoulos, who always gave people a sense of respect and dignity. He had a full forehead and well-groomed white beard, and his behavior exuded a kind of benevolence akin to a missionary.
[97] "Welcome, my dear friend!" Mr. Papapopoulos said in French.
[98] His tone was full of artificial sweetness.
[99] "Forgive me!" The visitor said, "It's so late to disturb you."
[100] "Not at all," Mr. Papapopoulos continued, "For tonight, this is a very interesting time. I guess you must have also had a very interesting night."
[101] "Not for me personally," the Count replied.
[102] "For you personally…" Mr. Papapopoulos repeated. "No, not that. So, do you have any news for me?"
[103] He asked this while giving the visitor a sharp glance, one that was neither holy nor friendly.
[104] "No news. Our plan failed. That's all I can report to you."
[105] "Indeed," Mr. Papapopoulos said. "Any violent action—"
[106] He waved his hand, indicating his extreme aversion to any form of violence. Indeed, in Mr. Papapoulos' daily life and business, there was no need for violence. He was well-known among European royalty, affectionately referred to as "Demetrius" (Demetrius, a Greek philosopher who compiled the first collection of Aesop's Fables: "Assemblies of Aesop's Tales"), and he had a reputation in the industry for his keen judgment of antiquities. All of this aristocratic reputation helped him in many difficult deals.
[107] "Direct attacks—" Mr. Papapoulos shook his head, "Sometimes they may work, but more often than not, they don't."
[108] The Marquis shrugged.
[109] "But it saves time," he emphasized, "and even if it fails, we have no losses. I have another plan, another plan that won't fail."
[110] "Is that so?" said Mr. Papapoulos, eagerly looking at him.
[111] The visitor nodded slowly.
[112] "Because of your, uh, your reputation," the antiquities dealer said, "I have a lot of confidence in you."
[113] The Count smiled gently.
[114] "I can assure you," he murmured, "I will not disappoint your trust."
[115] "I know you have unique conditions," Mr. Papapoulias said, his tone filled with undisguised envy.
[116] "It's all thanks to my own efforts," said the Marquis.
[117] He rose, picked up the coat he had casually hung on the back of the chair.
[118] "I will continue to contact you through the previous channels, Mr. Pappadopoulos. But our contract must not be affected or hindered in any way."
[119] This statement made Mr. Pappadopoulos look less than pleased.
[120] "My contracts are never altered," he complained.
[121] The Baron smiled, and without another word, he turned and left the room, closing the door behind him.
[122] Mr. Papapopoulos pondered for a moment, stroking his well-groomed white beard, and then turned to another door that stood ajar. As he turned the doorknob and pulled the door open, a young woman stumbled into the doorway, having apparently been listening at the keyhole, and hearing every word of the conversation. Mr. Papapopoulos showed no surprise or concern. It seemed he was accustomed to this.
[123] "Well, Zia, what do you want?" he asked.
[124] "I didn't hear him leave," Zia explained.
[125] She was a young, beautiful woman with graceful lines, her dark eyes shining. Her overall demeanor was so similar to that of Mr. Papapopoulos that it was easy to see that they were father and daughter.
[126] "It's so annoying," she continued angrily, "I can't see the room or hear the conversation from this keyhole."
[127] "It often frustrates me too," Pappadimos said candidly.
[128] "So, he's the Earl, then?" Zia said slowly. "Does he often wear a veil, daddy?"
[129] "Often."
[130] A brief pause followed.
[131] "I guess you're talking about the ruby, right?" Zia asked.
[132] Her father nodded.
[133] "What do you think, child?" he asked, his bright black eyes twinkling mischievously.
[134] "You mean the Marquis, right?"
[135] "Yes."
[136] "In my opinion," Zhiya said slowly, "it's not common for a native Englishman to speak French so fluently."
[137] "Oh, so that's what you think," said Mr. Papapoulos.
[138] As usual, he didn't express his opinion, but he showed some degree of agreement with his daughter's assessment.
[139] "I also think," said Zhiya, "that his head seems a bit deformed."
[140] "It's large," her father said. "His head is a bit too big. But that's usually the effect when people wear wigs."
[141] Father and daughter exchanged a glance and smiled knowingly.

[205] Chapter IV: On Kozens Street

[209] Ruth Kittling was twenty-eight years old, not beautiful, not particularly charming, but her various colors were certainly attractive. Von Alten was once called "carrot" and "ginger," and Ruth inherited his genes, with her auburn hair, dark eyes, and jet-black lashes, making her look like a work of art. She also dressed according to her colors. She had a slender figure and elegant posture, and at first glance, she looked like the Virgin Mary painted by Raphael. But if one looked closer at her face, they would see that she had the same square jawline as her father, which was suitable for a man but not as harmonious on a woman's face. From a young age, Ruth von Alten was accustomed to being headstrong, and if anyone dared to challenge her stubbornness, they would soon realize that Von Alten's daughter never gives in.

[1150] Chapter Thirteen: Telegram to von Alting
[1151] On the afternoon of February 15, London was shrouded in a thin yellow mist. In such weather, Rufus Von Alding continued to work tirelessly in his suite at the Savoy Hotel. In the past few days, Nayton had been quite happy because he found his boss a little absent-minded, and whenever he had the courage to urge Von Alding to deal with some urgent matters, he was met with a rather casual rejection. Now, Von Alding seemed to be putting twice as much effort into his work, and as his secretary, Nayton made the most of this time to work hard. Clever as Nayton was, he could always cover up his emotions without letting Von Alding notice anything unusual.

[1153] He listened intently to Nayton's report, but actually did not hear a single word. He nodded his head blankly, and when the secretary was about to pick up another file, Von Alding spoke:
[1154] "Could you tell me about that again, Nayton?"
[1155] The secretary was taken aback.

[1158] "I didn't make a mistake, sir. I actually spoke with her face to face."
[1159] "Well, tell me about the whole thing again."

[1161] "Well, well," said von Adler, "of course you would think they were together. And then the maid told you that Ruth continued on the train to the Riviera and left her at the hotel, waiting for further instructions?"
[1162] "Yes, that's right, sir."

[1164] "If that's the case," Neddy interjected, "then Mrs. Kettling should have sent her back to England, shouldn't she? It doesn't sound like something Mrs. Kettling would do, leaving her at the Ritz."
[1165] "Yes," the millionaire muttered, "that's right."
[1166] He wanted to say more, but did not. He liked and trusted Neddy, but he would not discuss his daughter's private matters with his secretary. Ruth's reticence had already hurt him, and this unexpected news only added to his concern.
[1167] Why had Ruth left the maid in Paris? What was her purpose or motive?
[1168] He considered all the possibilities. What could have happened to Ruth? She could not have expected the maid to run into her father's secretary so soon after arriving in Paris. The count knew his tricks wouldn't work on such a cunning Parisian woman. However, he could immediately conclude that the woman in front of him was extremely angry. And the count knew that when a woman was extremely angry, her caution would be thrown to the wind, which was a great advantage for a man who was her intellectual equal but more composed.
[1720] "You are too kind, madam. Your presence has made my humble abode radiant."
[1721] "We both have acquaintances in Paris," Milly said. "I've heard about your exploits from them, but I came to you today for something else. I heard, you know, about other things when I arrived in Nice."
[1722] "Is that so?" the Count asked softly.
[1723] "Forgive me, but what I'm about to say may not be pleasant to hear, but please believe me when I say I have your best interests at heart. Count, people in Nice are saying that you are the one who killed the British lady, the Katerina woman."
[1724] "Me? I am the one who killed Katerina? Ha! That's preposterous!"
[1725] His voice sounded indifferent rather than angry. He knew that this was the best way to get more information out of her.
[1726] "But that's what people are saying," she emphasized.
[1727] "People always like to spread rumors," the Count continued impassively. "If I were to take these rumors seriously, it would undermine my dignity."
[1728] "You misunderstand," said Milly, leaning forward and her black eyes shining. "This isn't some idle gossip on the street. This is from the police station."
[1729] "What? The police station?"
[1730] The Count suddenly stood up, his expression tense once again.
[1731] Mireille nodded repeatedly.
[1732] "Yes, that's right. You know me, I have friends everywhere. The police chief himself…" She shrugged meaningfully, without finishing her sentence.
[1733] "Who can keep a secret from a beauty?" the Count said politely.
[1734] "The police believe you killed the Countess Katerina. But they are mistaken."
[1735] "Of course they are mistaken," the Count agreed wholeheartedly.
[1736] "You just say it lightly, but I know the truth."
[1737] The Count looked at her with interest.
[1738] "Do you know who killed Lady Katerline?"
[1739] Mirella nodded vigorously.
[1740] "Yes."
[1741] "Then who is it?" the Count asked sternly.
[1742] "It was her own husband." She whispered in the Count's ear, her voice trembling with excitement and anger. "It was her husband who killed her."
[1743] The Count sat back in his chair, his face showing no emotion.
[1744] "May I ask, Miss, how do you know this?"
[1745] "How do I know this?" Mirella laughed and jumped up from her chair. "He threatened to do it himself. He was broke, in debt, and had no inheritance. The only way out for him was his wife's death. He told me so himself. He and Lady Katerlin were on the same train, but she had no idea. Tell me, why did he do it? Ah! So he could attack her in the middle of the night!"
[1746] The Count coughed.
[1747] "Perhaps so," he murmured. "But, Miss, you must understand, he had no need to steal the jewels as well."
[1748] "Jewels," Miranda gasped, "the jewels! Oh, those jewels!"
[1749] Her eyes filled with mist, which seemed to sparkle with light. The Count watched her in amazement, marveling for the hundredth time at the allure of jewelry to women. He had to bring her back to reality.
[1750] "So, what do you need me to do, Miss?"
[1751] Mireille snapped back to her professional demeanor.
[1752] "It's quite simple. You go to the police station and tell them that it was all the work of Mr. Ketling."
[1753] "But will they believe me if I show them evidence?"
[1754] Mireille smiled softly, wrapping herself tightly in her orange and black striped coat.
[1755] "Then let the police come to me, Count," she whispered. "I will give them the evidence."
[1756] With that, the woman left the room, as if she had completed her mission.
[1757] The Count raised his eyebrows and watched her leave.
[1758] "She's in a rage," he muttered to himself. "What could have made her so angry? But she did reveal all her cards. Does she really believe Kittling killed his wife? In any case, she wants me and the police to believe it."
[1759] He smiled to himself. He could think of many other ways to handle this, but he wasn't interested in going to the police station himself. From his smile, it seemed he had thought of a suitable solution.
[1760] However, a cloud soon passed over his face again. Miriam said the police suspected him. The news could be true or false, but the angry actress was unlikely to consider his perspective. On the other hand, this woman could easily obtain reliable first-hand information. If her information was accurate, he pursed his lips, he must take appropriate measures.
[1761] The Count returned to the house and asked Ipolite again if any strangers had come to inquire about him recently. The servant assured his master that such a thing had never happened. The Count went upstairs to his bedroom and approached an old desk placed against the wall. He removed the desktop and stretched out his long fingers to feel for a spring in one of the drawers. A secret drawer popped out, containing a small brown paper package. The Count took it out and held it in his hand, weighing it for a while. He plucked a hair from his head with one hand, wincing in pain as his features twisted. He placed the hair at the opening of the secret drawer and carefully locked it. He took the small package downstairs, went into the garage, and got into a bright red two-seater car. Ten minutes later, he was on his way to Monte Carlo.
[1762] He spent several hours in the casino and then strolled around town. He returned to the car and drove towards Menton (located in southeastern France, on the Mediterranean coast, bordering Italy). In the early afternoon, he noticed a nondescript gray car following him intermittently. Now he realized that the car was following him again. The road ahead was straight, and the Count stepped on the accelerator - the small car was custom-made for him, equipped with a powerful engine, with plenty of horsepower. Now the car was like an arrow shot from a bow, racing forward at high speed.
[1763] He glanced back and smiled: the gray car was still following him. The red car weaved through the dust at high speed. The speed was now dangerous, but the Count was an expert driver. Now it was downhill, with winding roads and sharp turns one after another. Suddenly the car began to slow down and finally stopped in front of a post office. The Count got out of the car, opened the trunk, took out the small paper package, and rushed into the post office. Two minutes later, he was back in the car, driving towards Menton. When the gray car arrived, the Count was already enjoying a cup of English afternoon tea on the balcony of a restaurant.
[1764] In the evening, he returned to Monte Carlo, had dinner there, and returned home around eleven o'clock. Ipolite opened the door to greet him, looking a little anxious.
[1765] "Ah! Count, you're finally back. Count, did you call me today?"
[1766] The count shook his head.
[1767] "At three o'clock this afternoon, I received orders to go to Nice and pick you up at Negresco."
[1768] "Is that so?" said the count. "So you went?"
[1769] "Of course, sir, but when I got there, the people at the hotel said they hadn't seen you."
[1770] "Well," said the count, "at that time, Mary was out shopping for dinner, was she?"
[1771] "Yes, Count Sir."
[1772] "Oh, well," said the Count, "nothing important, just a misunderstanding."
[1773] After finishing speaking, he went upstairs, smiling to himself.
[1774] As soon as he entered the bedroom, he immediately locked the door and carefully examined the surroundings. Everything seemed to be as usual. He opened all the drawers and cabinets, nodding his head, everything seemed to be untouched, but it was only "seemed" that way. It was evident that the entire room had been meticulously searched.
[1775] He walked up to the desk and pressed the hidden spring mechanism. The secret drawer popped out, but the hair was no longer in its original place. He nodded again.
[1776] "Our French police are quite impressive," he muttered to himself, "very impressive, nothing escapes their eyes."
[1777] Chapter XX: New Friends of Catherine

[3131] Chapter 34: The Blue Racer Again
[3132] The "Blue Racer" - sometimes called the "Millionaire's Express" - was speeding along the winding railway at a somewhat dangerous pace. Von Adler, Nayton, and Poirot sat quietly in the car. Nayton and Von Adler were in two adjoining compartments, the layout of which was identical to that of the one in which Ruth Kettering and her maid had stayed. Poirot's compartment was at the other end of the car.
[3133] The journey brought back painful memories for Von Adler. Poirot and Nayton occasionally exchanged a few quiet words, trying not to disturb him.
[3134] However, as the train approached the Lyon station, Poirot suddenly became lively. Von Adler realized that one of the purposes of the trip was to reenact the crime scene of that day. Poirot played multiple roles, going from a busy maid in his own compartment to a flustered Mrs. Kettering who recognized her husband, to a Mr. Derek Ketterlin who discovered his wife was also on the same train. He tried various possibilities, such as how to hide someone in the second compartment, and so on.
[3135] Suddenly, he seemed to have a flash of inspiration and grabbed Von Adler's arm.
[3136]"Oh, my God (French)," he exclaimed, "I forgot one thing! We must get off in Paris. Quick, quick, we must get off the train immediately."

[3184] Chapter 35: Poirot's Explanation
[3185] "Can you explain that?"
[3186] Poirot smiled slightly. He was sitting comfortably in an armchair, with his back to the millionaire's private room in the Negley's. From von Alten's expression, it was clear that he was both relaxed and curious. Poirot lit a thin cigarette and looked up at the ceiling.
[3187] "Well, let me explain. It was because of one thing that aroused my suspicion. Do you know what it was? It was the distorted face. In criminal investigations, this is a very common problem, an immediate question that arises, namely, the identification of the victim. I first thought of this too, was the victim really Lady Ketlin? But this question did not give me much inspiration, Miss Grey's testimony was so affirmative that it was impossible to doubt, so I put this idea aside, convinced that the victim was Ruth Ketlin."
[3188] "When did you begin to suspect the maid?"
[3189] "Just a short time ago, a trivial matter caught my attention. It was the cigarette case found in the compartment. According to her, it was a gift from Lady Ketlin to her husband. But I felt it was impossible, the couple had been estranged for a long time. This made me a little doubtful about Edith Mason's reliability. And she had only worked for Lady Ketlin for two months, which made her more suspicious. Of course, since she was left in Paris and Lady Ketlin was still seen by many people as being alive, she seemed to have nothing to do with the case."
[3190] Poirot stood up, pointing his index finger to the sky and shaking his head, looking at von Alten with a rich expression:
[3191] "However, I am an experienced detective. I am often suspicious. I doubt everyone and everything. I do not believe anything anyone tells me. I ask myself: How do we know that Ada Mason was indeed left in Paris? The initial answer seems flawless. That is the testimony of your secretary, Colonel Nayton, whose words are completely reliable as an outsider. Moreover, your daughter has spoken to the stewardess herself, making it even more difficult to doubt. However, I have temporarily put this point aside for now. A very possible and impractical idea comes to my mind, and if this idea is true, then the previous testimony can be proven to be meaningless."
[3192] "I concentrated on one scenario, that Colonel Neddy saw Mason at the Ritz Hotel in Paris just as the 'Blue Train' was leaving Paris. This seemed plausible, but after careful observation, I noticed two things: first, it was interesting that Colonel Neddy had only been working here for two months; second, the letters on the cigarette case matched the first letter of his name. Then, if Ada Mason was an accomplice, her explanation would make sense. First – let's review the whole case – she quickly concocted a plausible alibi for Mr. Kertling's false charges. This was not part of the plan, and she was too clever to do so. They wanted the Count de Rochefort to be the scapegoat, so Ada Mason emphasized that she wasn't entirely sure if the figure she saw was the Count. Now, if you recall, there was an incident where I told Ada Mason that the figure she saw might not have been the Count de Rochefort but Derek Kertling. She seemed uncertain at first, but after I returned to the hotel, you called to tell me that she had told you, after careful consideration, that the figure was indeed Mr. Kertlin. I found this strange. From her perspective, there was only one plausible explanation for this change. After leaving your residence, she discussed the entire situation with someone and received some advice, which led her to act accordingly. Who gave her this advice? Of course, it was Colonel Neddy. And there's a seemingly insignificant point: Colonel Neddy had casually mentioned a jewel heist that occurred in Yorkshire, where he was present. It could have been a coincidence, or it could have been a small piece in the puzzle."
[3193] "But there is one thing I don't understand, Mr. Poirot. I apologize for my dullness, but I still can't figure out who the man at the Paris station was. Was it Derek Catterall or Count Roger?"
[3194] "This answer is very simple. There is no such man. Oh! It's outrageous! Do you see the cleverest part of this whole case? Who told us there was such a man? Only Ada Mason saw him. And we only believed Ada Mason because Neidt said he saw her left in Paris."
[3195] "But Ruth had told the stewardess herself that she left the maid in Paris," von Ahring interrupted him.
[3196] "I was just about to explain this. We do have a statement from Mrs. Ketling, but from another perspective, we do not have her actual statement. Von Alding, the deceased cannot speak to us. This is not her statement, but rather the statement of the stewardess, which are two different concepts.
[3197] "So, you think the stewardess is lying?"
[3198] "No, no, he is not lying. He is stating what he believes to be true. But the person who told him that the maid was left in Paris was not Mrs. Ketling.
[3199] Von Alding looked at him in confusion.
[3200] "Von Alding, Mrs. Ruth Ketling was already dead before the train reached Lyon. Edith Mason wore the mistress's clothes to buy dinner and told the stewardess that crucial sentence."
[3201] "This is almost unbelievable!"
[3202] "No, no, Mr. Von Alting. This is not impossible. Women today look so much alike that people often identify them by their clothes rather than their faces. Edith Mason is almost the same height as your daughter. Dressed in such expensive fur coats and wearing a hat that covers half her face, it would be easy to pass off as the mistress, and you remember, the stewardess had not spoken to Mrs. Katelyn before this. Yes, he saw the maid at the ticket check, but at that time, in his memory, there was only a weary-looking woman in black clothes. Unless he is extremely clever, he would not be able to discover the change in identity between the mistress and the maid. Please do not forget, Edith Mason's real name is Kitty Kid, an actress, so she is skilled at changing her appearance and voice. No, there is no danger of the stewardess recognizing the disguised maid. However, there is another danger: that he will discover that the person he saw was not the same woman who spoke to him the night before. That's why they disfigured the corpse. The only threat to these criminals is Miss Katherine Grey. If she visits the lady's compartment again after the train leaves Paris, it would be extremely unfavorable for them. For this reason, the criminals came up with a trick: they bought dinner and locked the compartment."
[3203] "Who killed my poor Ruth? When?"
[3204] "Firstly, this crime was planned by two people - Nayton and Edith Mason. Nayton was in Paris that day to do something for you. He jumped onto the train near the Paris suburban railway. Mrs. Katelyn was surprised to see Nayton, but she would not have suspected anything. He might have given her an excuse to look out the window, and then he strangled her from behind with a rope. A few seconds later, Mrs. Katelyn was no more. They locked the compartment door and began their cleanup work: taking off the dead woman's outerwear, wrapping the body in a blanket, and placing it between the packages and suitcases in the adjacent compartment. Nayton took the jewelry box and jumped off the train. Since everyone believed that the death occurred around midnight, he was absolutely safe. His testimony, along with the supposed conversation between Mrs. Katelyn and the stewardess, created a perfect 'alibi' for him."
[3205] "In Paris, at Lyon station, Edith Mason returned to the compartment with the dinner box, locked the door, and as quickly as possible changed into the mistress's clothes, putting on two golden curls prepared in advance and doing her makeup to resemble the mistress as much as possible. When the steward came to make the bed, she told the story of leaving her maid in Paris. While the steward made the bed, she stood by the window looking out, her back to the corridor, where there were passing travelers. This was a very clever precaution, as there might have been Miss Grey among the travelers; if she saw her, she could swear that Mrs. Katelyn was still alive."
[3206] "Please continue," said Mr. Von Alting.
[3207] "Before the train arrived in Lyon, Ada Mason placed the mistress's body on the bed, neatly folded her outer clothes and placed them at the foot of the bed, then changed into male clothes and prepared to disembark. When Mr. Derrick Kate entered his wife's compartment, he thought his wife was sleeping, providing another witness, while Mason hid in the next compartment, waiting to sneak off the train. At Lyon station, she followed the steward down and pretended to be a passenger going outside to breathe fresh air. While people were not paying attention, she quickly went to another platform and boarded the first train to Paris, returning to the Ritz Hotel. Her name had been registered the day before by one of Nayton's accomplices at the hotel. After that, she spent her time at the hotel, waiting for the police to come knocking. The jewelry, of course, was not on her hands. No one suspected Nayton. He, as your secretary, had safely brought the jewelry to Nice. The transaction of the jewelry in Nice with Papapoulos was something that had been planned in advance and ultimately carried out by Ada Mason. Overall, this conspiracy was well-executed. Only a connoisseur like the Marquis could do it justice!"
[3208] "Are you sure Richard Nayton is the notorious criminal who has been committing crimes for the past few years?"
[3209] Poirot nodded.
[3210] "The so-called Marquis has two seductive weapons: flattery and eloquence. That
[3211] "I swear he didn't seem eager to take this job at the time," the millionaire exclaimed.
[3212] "This man is cunning and far-sighted, and his mastery of interpersonal relationships may be on par with yours, Mr. von Arding."
[3213] "I have also investigated his background, and everyone vouches for him being a good person."
[3214] "Of course, that's part of the plot. Richard Nayton's life record is spotless. He comes from a good family, lives a comfortable and happy life, and shows bravery and loyalty during wartime. When I began investigating the mysterious Marquis, I found some similarities between him and Nayton. Nayton speaks fluent French, just like a real Frenchman, and the time he spent in America, France, and Britain coincides with the 'working time' of the Marquis. The Marquis' last appearance was in a major jewelry theft case in Switzerland, where you, sir, met Colonel Nayton. It was also at that time that some insiders leaked the news that you were going to buy that valuable gemstone."
[3215] "But why kill someone?" mumbled von Arding. "A master criminal would not put himself on the chopping block."
[3216] Poirot shook his head. "This is not the first time the count has caused bloodshed. He is a sadistic murderer. Moreover, in order to ensure that there is no evidence, he does not want to leave any evidence, and dead people cannot speak."
[3217] "The count has an uncontrollable passion for precious and historically valuable gems. When he planned to become your secretary, he began to scheme with your daughter's maid on how to harm your daughter, as he guessed that the gem would eventually belong to Ruth Kittling. Moreover, he tried to take a shortcut. Therefore, he hired a few thugs to carry out an attack on the night you bought the gem. This plan failed, but the count did not feel sudden and disappointed. I believe he thought the small case was done very well and no one would suspect him. And like all big people - I should say the count is indeed a big man - they all have their weaknesses. He truly fell in love with Miss Gray, and when he discovered that she liked Derek Kittling a little, he unconsciously and instinctively tried to frame Derek. Now, Mr. von Adler, I will tell you something very interesting: Miss Gray, although not a neurotic person, once felt that your daughter was by her side in the park of the Monte Carlo casino, and she had just finished a long conversation with Colonel Newton. According to her, the deceased wanted to tell her something urgently, and suddenly she felt that the deceased wanted to say: Colonel Newton is the murderer! This idea seemed too unrealistic at the time, so Miss Gray did not tell anyone about it. But she was so curious about the truth that she took a nearly crazy action. She did not refuse Colonel Newton's pursuit and pretended to have accepted the fact that Derek Kittling was the criminal."
[3218] "It's too bizarre!" said von Adler.
[3219] "Yes, very strange. It's always difficult to explain such things. By the way, there's one small thing that made me doubtful. Due to the injuries he suffered during the war, your secretary is slightly lame. But the count walks without a limp. I couldn't figure this out for a long time. One day, Miss Reynolds Temple casually mentioned that the orthopedic surgeon at her mother's hospital found Newton's limp strange. This suggests that his limp is likely to be fake. I found an orthopedic expert in London and obtained specialized materials that proved my idea was correct. I mentioned the name of the doctor in front of Newton. He should have spoken up if he had been treated by that doctor during the war. But he didn't say a word about it, and this small detail deepened my suspicions. Moreover, Miss Gray showed me a clipping that mentioned a gem theft incident at Mrs. Temple's hospital during Newton's hospitalization. At that time, she realized that when I wrote her a letter from the Ritz Hotel in Paris, we were investigating in the same direction."
[3220] "Although I made great efforts, I finally obtained the evidence that Adah Mason arrived at the hotel on the morning after the murder, not the night before."
[3221] The two fell silent for a long time. Then the millionaire extended his hand across the table to Poirot.
[3222] "You may know what this means to me, Poirot, sir. Later, I will give you a check, but there is no check in the world that can express my gratitude to you. You are really great, Poirot, sir, no matter when you are an expert in this field.
[3223] Poirot stood up, straightened his back.
[3224] "I am just Hercule Poirot." He said cautiously, "But as you said, I am an expert in my field, just as you are an expert in yours. I am glad to be of service to you. Now I will do some post-travel cleanup. Oh, I forgot to bring my George, the understanding one.
[3225] In the hotel lobby, he met the stern-faced Papadopoulos and his daughter Zia.
[3226] "I thought you had already left Nice, Mr. Polo," the Greek man whispered to the detective, taking his hand.
[3227] "Business brought me back, my dear Papadimos."
[3228] "Business?"
[3229] "Yes, business. Since we're on the subject, I hope your health has improved, Papadimos."
[3230] "Much better, actually. We'll be returning to Paris tomorrow."
[3231] "I'm glad to hear that. I hope you haven't ruined the former Greek prime minister with your actions."
[3232] "Me?"
[3233] "I heard you sold him a very valuable gemstone, which is now worn by the dancer Mirella. And only you and I know this."
[3234] "Yes," Papadimos murmured, "yes, that's true."
[3235] "It's a gemstone very similar to the 'Flame Heart'."
[3236] "There is a slight difference," the Greek man said nonchalantly.
[3237] "Mr. Pappadimos, you certainly know a lot about jewelry. Miss Zia, your quick return to Paris makes me feel particularly sad. Now that my business is done, I had hoped we could spend more time together."
[3238] "May I ask, what business did you have?" Pappadimos asked.
[3239] "No problem, just a casual question. I have just successfully captured the Marquis."
[3240] Pappadimos' aristocratic face showed a dazed expression.
[3241] "Marquis?" He murmured, "Why does that name sound so familiar? Ah, I can't quite remember."
[3242] "You wouldn't know him," Bello said. "I am referring to a notorious murder case and a jewel thief. He was arrested for the murder of Lady Katerina."
[3243] "Is that so? That is quite interesting!"
[3244] They then parted politely. After Bello walked away, Papadopoulos turned to his daughter and said:
[3245] "Zia," he sighed emotionally, "this man is a devil."

[3248] Chapter Thirty-six: On the Coast
[3249] The hibiscus flowers had fallen, and the bougainvillea surrounded Templeton's villa, while the sweet scent of jasmine filled the air. The Mediterranean was bluer than ever. Poirot and Miss Reynolds sat on the terrace. He had just finished telling her the story of the mysterious figure - "Duke" - the same story he had told Mr. Vandermarck two days earlier. Miss Reynolds listened intently, her brow furrowed and her expression somber.
[3250] When Poirot finished, she asked simply:
[3251] "What about Derek?"
[3252] "He was released yesterday."
[3253] "Where did he go?"
[3254] "He left Nice last night."
[3255] "To Saint-Mary-Mead village?"
[3256] "Yes."
[3257] There was a silence.
[3258] "I misunderstood Katherine," said Renois. "I thought she didn't care about Derek."
[3259] "She doesn't trust anyone, she's reserved with everyone."
[3260] "She could have trusted me," Renois said in a pained tone.
[3261] "Yes," said Poirot gravely. "She could have trusted you. But Miss Katherine has spent most of her life listening to others, and people who are used to listening find it difficult to speak of themselves. They hide their joys and sorrows, keeping them to themselves, not sharing them with others."
[3262] "I was so foolish," said Reneau. "I thought she might have fallen in love with Nayton. I should have known her better. I suppose I thought that because...well, it was just wishful thinking."
[3263] Poirot took her hand, gently holding it, and spoke in a friendly and gentle tone: "You need to muster your courage, Miss. You need to be strong."
[3264] Reneau stared off into the distance, her plain and serious face suddenly taking on a melancholic beauty.
[3265] "Oh dear," she said. "The outcome is so tragic. I'm too young for Derek, he seems like a child who will never grow up. He needs someone like the Virgin Mary."
[3266] There was a long silence, and then Leno suddenly turned to the detective. "But I did help you, Mr. Poirot, in some small way."
[3267] "Indeed, Miss. It was through you that I got a clue to the truth. You pointed out that the killer might not have been a passenger on the train. Before that, I was at a loss."
[3268] Leno took a deep breath.
[3269] "Well, I'm glad of that."
[3270] In the distance, the sound of a train's whistle could be heard, its tone drawn out.
[3271] "That's the blasted 'Blue Express', " Renos said. "Trains are such cold-hearted things, aren't they, Mr. Poirot? People are murdered on them, they die on them, and the trains keep on going. Oh, I'm rambling again, but you know what I mean."
[3272] "Yes, I do. Life is like a train, Miss. It keeps moving forward. And that's what makes it so fascinating."
[3273] "Why?"
[3274] "Because a train journey always has an end. And there's a saying in your language that goes along with that."
[3275] " 'Wanderlust ends at the meeting of lovers.' " Renos grinned. "But that doesn't apply to me."
[3276] "It fits, of course it fits. You are young; you are younger than you think you are. Believe in the train, young lady; you must believe in the train that is driven by God."
[3277] The whistle of the train sounded once more.
[3278] "Believe in the train, mademoiselle." Poirot whispered again, "Trust Hercule Poirot, he knows everything."
